Now, the sequence: For a given a1, we iterate f. The condition that each term has at least three proper divisors means that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors (i.e., primes or prime squares). So we need to find all a1 for which the orbit under f avoids these "bad" numbers. Also we need the sequence to be infinite; but if it eventually becomes periodic with a cycle that does not contain bad numbers, that's fine. Possibly some a1 lead to a fixed point or a cycle, and others lead to a bad number eventually.

But note: The problem statement: "The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1." This means that we are to find all a_1 such that there exists an infinite sequence (i.e., the sequence can be defined for all n) and each term satisfies the condition. So we need to characterize the initial term that allows the process to continue indefinitely without violating the condition.

Thus the problem reduces to: Determine all positive integers N such that the sequence defined by a_1 = N, a_{n+1}=f(a_n) has the property that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. Let's denote the set of numbers that have at least three proper divisors as T = { n : d(n) ≥ 4 }. The function f: T → N (but note that f(N) might not be in T; if f(N) is prime or prime square, then the sequence would stop, but we require that all terms stay in T). So we are looking for N such that f^k(N) ∈ T for all k. Since f is defined on all integers with at least three proper divisors, but the output may not satisfy the condition. So we need the orbit to remain in T.

Observation: For numbers that are multiples of 6, f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N if N not divisible by 4 or 5? Actually the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6 if N is divisible by 6 and not by 4 or 5. Maybe we can prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then eventually the sequence hits a prime or prime square. Let's explore the behavior of f on multiples of 6. Let N = 6m. Write the prime factorization. The smallest divisor >1 is 2 (since even). But careful: If N is divisible by 2 and 3, it is divisible by 6, so 6 is always a divisor. However, if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor and is less than 6, so the set of divisors >1 includes 2,3,4 (and possibly others). So the third smallest is 4 (provided no other divisor like 5? But 5>4, so 4 is third). If N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then the divisors >1 include 2,3,5,6,... So the third smallest is 5. If N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then the divisors >1 include 2,3,6,... So the third smallest is 6. Thus for N=6m, we have:

- If m is even (i.e., N divisible by 4), then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12? Actually compute: N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12.
- If m is divisible by 5 (i.e., N divisible by 5) but m odd (so not divisible by 2? Actually if m divisible by 5, then N=6m divisible by 5. Could also be divisible by 4? That case already covered. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (15N+10N+6N)/30 = 31N/30.
- If m is odd and not divisible by 5, then N not divisible by 4 or 5. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So N is fixed.

Thus for any multiple of 6, f(N) = 13N/12 if N divisible by 4, = 31N/30 if N divisible by 5 but not by 4, = N if N not divisible by 4 or 5. So this classification is based on the exponent of 2 and factor 5.

But note: If N is divisible by 4, then it's divisible by 2^2. So the exponent of 2 is at least 2. In that case, the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4? But is it possible that N is divisible by 4 and also by 5? Then N divisible by 4 and 5, then the three smallest divisors >1: 2,3,4? Since 4<5, yes. So f=13N/12. But note: The sequence is infinite if and only if every term is in T. Let's attempt to find a counterexample. Suppose N is even and not divisible by 3. Write N = 2^e * m, with m odd and gcd(m,3)=1. Then the three smallest divisors >1: if e=1, then p=2, q = smallest odd divisor (≥5), r = next odd divisor. Then f = N/2 + N/q + N/r = 2^(e-1)*m + (2^e*m)/q + (2^e*m)/r. So for f to be divisible by 3, we need f ≡ 0 mod 3. But we can compute f mod 3. Since N not divisible by 3, all divisors are not multiples of 3. Since m is not divisible by 3, we need to check if this can be 0. For e=1, f is odd, so cannot be divisible by 6 because it's not even. So we only need to consider e≥2. Then f ≡ (-1)^(e-1)*m + (-1)^e*(m/q+m/r) = (-1)^(e-1)*m + (-1)^e*(m/q+m/r) = (-1)^(e-1)[m - (m/q+m/r)] mod3. Since (-1)^(e-1) is ±1, f divisible by 3 iff m - (m/q+m/r) ≡ 0 mod3. So we need m ≡ m/q+m/r (mod3). Since m is not divisible by 3, we can multiply by the inverse of m mod3. So 1 ≡ 1/q+1/r (mod3). We define \(T=\{N\in\mathbb{Z}^+: d(N)\ge 4\}\) (numbers with at least three proper divisors) and for \(N\in T\) let  
\(f(N)\) be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence satisfies \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) with each \(a_n\in T\).

**Lemma 1.** If \(N\in T\) and \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\) and the iterates \(N, f(N), f(f(N)),\dots\) strictly decrease until they reach a number not in \(T\).

*Proof.* Write the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\) as \(p\le q\le r\). Then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/p, N/q, N/r\), so \(f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r\).

If \(p=2\) (so \(N\) even) then \(N\) is not divisible by \(3\). The second smallest divisor \(q\) is either \(4\) (if \(N\) is divisible by \(4\)) or an odd prime \(\ge5\). In either case we obtain  
\(f(N)\le \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{8}=\frac{7N}{8}<N\) (if \(N\) divisible by \(4\)) or  
\(f(N)\le \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{25}=\frac{36N}{50}<N\) (if \(N\equiv2\pmod4\)).  

If \(p\ge5\) (so \(N\) odd) then \(f(N)\le \frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}=\frac{3N}{5}<N\).  
Thus in all cases \(f(N)<N\). The sequence is strictly decreasing; because it consists of positive integers it must eventually reach a minimum. If that minimum were in \(T\) it would be a fixed point, but for \(N\in T\) not a multiple of \(6\) we have \(f(N)<N\), so no fixed point exists. Hence the decreasing sequence cannot stay in \(T\) forever; it must hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors. ∎

**Consequence.** For the infinite sequence to exist with all terms in \(T\), \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\). Indeed, if \(a_1\) were not a multiple of \(6\), Lemma 1 would force the sequence to eventually leave \(T\), contradicting the definition. So \(a_1\in6T\).
